<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genomes shed light on early angiosperm evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2020 6:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-02-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Angiosperms represent one of the most spectacular terrestrial radiations on the planet1, but their early diversification and phylogenetic relationships remain uncertain2&#8211;5. A key reason for this impasse is the paucity of complete genomes representing early-diverging angiosperms. Here, we present high-quality, chromosomal-level genome assemblies of two aquatic species&#8212;prickly waterlily (Euryale ferox; Nymphaeales) and the rigid hornwort (Ceratophyllum demersum; Ceratophyllales)&#8212;and expand the genomic representation for key sectors of the angiosperm tree of life. We identify multiple independent polyploidization events in each of the five major clades (that is, Nymphaeales, magnoliids, monocots, Ceratophyllales and eudicots). Furthermore, our phylogenomic analyses, which spanned multiple datasets and diverse methods, confirm that Amborella and Nymphaeales are successively sister to all other angiosperms. Furthermore, these genomes help to elucidate relationships among the major subclades within Mesangiospermae, which contain about 350,000 species. In particular, the species-poor lineage Ceratophyllales is supported as sister to eudicots, and monocots and magnoliids are placed as successively sister to Ceratophyllales and eudicots. Finally, our analyses indicate that incomplete lineage sorting may account for the incongruent phylogenetic placement of magnoliids between nuclear and plastid genomes. Genome assemblies of two aquatic species, prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort, clarify the early diversification and phylogeny of plants. Multiple independent polyploidization events are inferred in each of the five major angiosperm clades."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-8755-6364"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-8755-6364</a></span><sup /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click"             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-2851-5474"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-2851-5474</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Sichuan University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0807 1581, grid.13291.38, Key Laboratory of Bio-Resource and Eco-Environment of Ministry of Education &amp; State Key Laboratory of Hydraulics &amp; Mountain River Engineering, College of Life Sciences, Sichuan University, Chengdu, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Xiyin-Wang" data-author-popup="auth-Xiyin-Wang" data-corresp-id="c2">Xiyin Wang<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8747-1101"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8747-1101</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Harvard University Herbaria" /><meta itemprop="address" content="000000041936754X, grid.38142.3c, Department of Organismic and Evolutionary Biology, Harvard University Herbaria, Cambridge, MA, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jianquan-Liu" data-author-popup="auth-Jianquan-Liu" data-corresp-id="c4">Jianquan Liu<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Angiosperms represent one of the most spectacular terrestrial radiations on the planet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Judd, W. S., Campbell, C. S., Kellogg, E. A., Stevens, P. F. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Plant Systematics (Sinauer Sunderland, 2002)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12344e629">1</a></sup>, but their early diversification and phylogenetic relationships remain uncertain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Friedman, W. E. The meaning of Darwin’s ‘abominable mystery’. Am. J. Bot. 96, 5–21 (2009)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12344e633">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Buggs, R. J. A. The deepening of Darwin’s abominable mystery. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0169 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12344e633_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12344e633_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Steemans, P. et al. Origin and radiation of the earliest vascular land plants. Science 324, 353 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12344e636">5</a></sup>. A key reason for this impasse is the paucity of complete genomes representing early-diverging angiosperms. Here, we present high-quality, chromosomal-level genome assemblies of two aquatic species—prickly waterlily (<i>Euryale ferox</i>; Nymphaeales) and the rigid hornwort (<i>Ceratophyllum demersum</i>; Ceratophyllales)—and expand the genomic representation for key sectors of the angiosperm tree of life. We identify multiple independent polyploidization events in each of the five major clades (that is, Nymphaeales, magnoliids, monocots, Ceratophyllales and eudicots). Furthermore, our phylogenomic analyses, which spanned multiple datasets and diverse methods, confirm that <i>Amborella</i> and Nymphaeales are successively sister to all other angiosperms. Furthermore, these genomes help to elucidate relationships among the major subclades within Mesangiospermae, which contain about 350,000 species. In particular, the species-poor lineage Ceratophyllales is supported as sister to eudicots, and monocots and magnoliids are placed as successively sister to Ceratophyllales and eudicots. Finally, our analyses indicate that incomplete lineage sorting may account for the incongruent phylogenetic placement of magnoliids between nuclear and plastid genomes.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The angiosperms, or flowering plants, represent one of the most diverse and species-rich clades on Earth. They provide the vast majority of food consumed by humans and contribute substantially to global photosynthesis and carbon sequestration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Judd, W. S., Campbell, C. S., Kellogg, E. A., Stevens, P. F. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Plant Systematics (Sinauer Sunderland, 2002)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12344e671">1</a></sup>. The origin of angiosperms was famously coined ‘an abominable mystery’ owing to their sudden appearance and rapid diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Friedman, W. E. The meaning of Darwin’s ‘abominable mystery’. Am. J. Bot. 96, 5–21 (2009)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12344e675">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Buggs, R. J. A. The deepening of Darwin’s abominable mystery. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0169 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12344e675_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12344e675_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Steemans, P. et al. Origin and radiation of the earliest vascular land plants. Science 324, 353 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12344e678">5</a></sup>. To date, angiosperms include more than 350,000 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="The Plant List. The Plant List – A working list of all plant species. Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew and Missouri Botanical Garden (2019). Available online at &#xA;http://www.theplantlist.org/&#xA;&#xA; (last retrieved 20 Aug 2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d12344e682">6</a></sup> and occupy nearly every habitat from forests and grasslands to sea margins and deserts; angiosperms encompass a considerable variety of life forms, including trees, herbs, submerged aquatics and epiphytes. Resolving early angiosperm phylogeny is therefore critical for our understanding of such diversifying processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Judd, W. S., Campbell, C. S., Kellogg, E. A., Stevens, P. F. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Plant Systematics (Sinauer Sunderland, 2002)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d12344e686">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d12344e689">7</a></sup>.</p><p>Decades of efforts have greatly resolved the angiosperm phylogeny, illuminating their evolutionary history and helping to delineate major groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Friedman, W. E. The meaning of Darwin’s ‘abominable mystery’. Am. J. Bot. 96, 5–21 (2009)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d12344e696">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Buggs, R. J. A. The deepening of Darwin’s abominable mystery. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0169 (2017)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d12344e696_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12344e696_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Steemans, P. et al. Origin and radiation of the earliest vascular land plants. Science 324, 353 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d12344e699">5</a></sup>. It has been identified that the three early-diverging angiosperm orders Amborellales, Nymphaeales and Austrobaileyales, which constitute remarkable morphological disparity and low species diversity, represent the earliest diverged angiosperm lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Qiu, Y.-L. et al. The earliest angiosperms: evidence from mitochondrial, plastid and nuclear genomes. Nature 402, 404–407 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d12344e703">8</a></sup> (that is, the so-called ANA grade). However, the vast majority of angiosperms belong to the Mesangiospermae clade, which includes approximately 99% of all extant angiosperms. Eudicots and monocots are the two largest Mesangiospermae subclades, including around 75% and 22% of all species, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Drinnan, A. N., Crane, P. R. &amp; Hoot, S. B. in Early Evolution of Flowers Supplement 8, Vol. 8 (eds Endress, P. K. &amp; Friis, E. M.) 93–122 (Springer, 1994)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d12344e707">9</a></sup>; magnoliids represent a third subclade with about 9,000 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cronquist, A. &amp; Takhtadzhian, A. L. An Integrated System of Classification of Flowering Plants (Columbia Univ. Press, 1981)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12344e711">10</a></sup>; and the remaining two subclades, Chloranthales and Ceratophyllales, are morphologically unusual with only 77 and 7 species, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cronquist, A. &amp; Takhtadzhian, A. L. An Integrated System of Classification of Flowering Plants (Columbia Univ. Press, 1981)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d12344e715">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Friis, E. M., Pedersen, K. R. &amp; Crane, P. R. Diversity in obscurity: fossil flowers and the early history of angiosperms. Proc. R. Soc. B 365, 369–382 (2010)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d12344e715_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dilcher, D. L. &amp; Wang, H. An early cretaceous fruit with affinities to Ceratophyllaceae. Am. J. Bot. 96, 2256–2269 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d12344e718">12</a></sup>. Despite the elucidation and the strong support for each of the five subclades of Mesangiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12344e723">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12344e726">13</a></sup>, phylogenetic relationships among these clades remain uncertain, and different topologies have been proposed on the basis of various morphological<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Endress, P. K. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Reconstructing the ancestral angiosperm flower and its initial specializations. Am. J. Bot. 96, 22–66 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d12344e730">14</a></sup> and/or molecular lines of evidence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chase, M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d12344e734">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Moore, M. J., Bell, C. D., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Using plastid genome-scale data to resolve enigmatic relationships among basal angiosperms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 19363–19368 (2007)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d12344e737">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yin-Long, Q. et al. Angiosperm phylogeny inferred from sequences of four mitochondrial genes. J. Sys. Evol. 48, 391–425 (2010)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d12344e737_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, N., Zeng, L., Shan, H. &amp; Ma, H. Highly conserved low‐copy nuclear genes as effective markers for phylogenetic analyses in angiosperms. N. Phytol. 195, 923–937 (2012)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d12344e737_2">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12344e740">18</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>Genomic data provide a rich and convincing means to resolve such evolutionary uncertainties. Despite the availability of numerous sequenced genomes from eudicots and monocots, early-diverging angiosperms remain poorly sampled, therefore inhibiting insights into these fundamental questions. To date, no nuclear genome has been sequenced for the four key orders—Austrobaileyales, Ceratophyllales, Chloranthales and Nymphaeales—which exhibit diverse life histories, extreme morphological variation and great evolutionary divergence. This lack of critical taxon sampling probably exacerbates phylogenetic uncertainty when inferring early angiosperm relationships. For example, nuclear genomes of three magnoliids (that is, <i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i>, <i>Liriodendron chinense</i> and <i>Persea americana</i>) have been subsequently published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S.-M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12344e759">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species–pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2019)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12344e759_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rendon-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12344e762">21</a></sup>; however, phylogenetic analyses in these two studies resulted in conflicting placement of magnoliids relative to monocots and eudicots—that is, either monocots as the sister to a clade of magnoliids and eudicots, or magnoliids as the sister to monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S.-M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12344e766">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species–pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2019)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12344e766_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rendon-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12344e766_2">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6–7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12344e769">22</a></sup>. Moreover, cases of deep phylogenetic incongruence between nuclear and organellar genomes have been recently reported in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S.-M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63–73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species–pair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18–25 (2019)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774_2">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rendon-Anaya, M. et al. The avocado genome informs deep angiosperm phylogeny, highlights introgressive hybridization, and reveals pathogen-influenced gene space adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 17081–17089 (2019)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774_3">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6–7 (2019)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774_4">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d12344e774_5">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sun, M. et al. Deep phylogenetic incongruence in the angiosperm clade Rosidae. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 83, 156–166 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d12344e777">24</a></sup>, but their causation (such as hybridization and incomplete lineage sorting (ILS)) has not been fully evaluated.</p><p>Here we report the high-quality chromosomal-level genome assemblies of <i>E. ferox</i> Salisb. (prickly waterlily; estimated genome size of 768.2 Mb) and <i>C. demersum</i> L. (rigid hornwort; estimated genome size of 777.2 Mb), which are representatives of the two aquatic lineages Nymphaeales and Ceratophyllales, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">1</a>). A total of 31.7 Gb of Oxford Nanopore Technologies (ONT) long reads and 47.4 Gb of Illumina short reads were generated for <i>Euryale</i>, and 80.5 Gb of ONT long reads and 46.4 Gb of short reads were generated for <i>Ceratophyllum</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">2</a>). ONT long reads were de novo assembled into contigs using the Canu assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12344e809">25</a></sup>, and two rounds of polishing were applied to the assembled contigs using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12344e813">26</a></sup> with the Illumina short reads. The resulting genome assemblies of <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i> were 725.2 Mb (N50 size of 4.75 Mb, where N50 corresponds to the minimum contig length needed to cover 50% of the genome) and 733.3 Mb (N50 size of 1.56 Mb), respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">3</a>). Moreover, a total of 84.4 Gb and 133.9 Gb of Hi-C data were generated using the Illumina platform for <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively. Assembled contigs were then clustered into 29 and 12 pseudochromosomes for <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively, using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12344e840">27</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">4</a>). Both genome assemblies showed a high contiguity, completeness and accuracy (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">5</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">7</a>), and matched the chromosome counts obtained from cytological studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Rice, A. et al. The Chromosome Counts Database (CCDB)—a community resource of plant chromosome numbers. N. Phytol. 206, 19–26 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d12344e866">28</a></sup>. Using a combination of homology-based and transcriptome-based approaches, 40,297 and 30,138 protein-coding genes were predicted in the genomes of <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">5</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">8</a>). Moreover, 78.3% and 71.2% of all of the predicted protein-coding genes were clustered into gene families for <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">9</a>), and 85.6% and 89.8% of all of the predicted protein-coding genes were successfully annotated by at least one database (that is, SwissProt, TrEMBL, InterPro, GO or KEGG) for <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">10</a>). Furthermore, despite the similar genome size of these two species, the percentage of predicted repetitive elements was much higher in the genome of <i>Ceratophyllum</i> (that is, 38.35% versus 63.08% for <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparative genomics analyses."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Comparative genomics analyses.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="888" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The genomic features of <i>E. ferox</i> (pseudomolecules size: 721.2 Mb) and <i>C. demersum</i> (pseudomolecules size: 703.8 Mb). From inside to outside: GC content in 500-bp sliding windows (i; minimum–maximum, 0.2–0.8); repeat density in 10-kb sliding windows (ii; minimum–maximum: 0–1.0, coloured from white to dark green); gene density in 100-kb sliding windows (iii; minimum–maximum, 0–30, coloured from white to dark orange); and SNV density in 100-kb sliding windows (iv; minimum–maximum: 0–0.025). The links in the centre connect syntenic gene blocks that were detected using MCscan. Chr, chromosome. <b>b</b>, Distribution of average synonymous substitution levels (Ks) between syntenic blocks after evolutionary rate correction. <b>c</b>, Syntenic blocks (involving ≥10 colinear genes) between genomes. The corresponding median Ks value is shown for each block, and polyploidization events are represented by different colours.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>By constructing the distribution of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) using syntenic paralogues within each genome, we detected two and three polyploidization events in the genomes of <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">7</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">12</a>). After correction for evolutionary rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wang, X. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinform. 7, 447 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d12344e960">29</a></sup>, the two polyploidization events in the genome of <i>Euryale</i> were estimated to occur at approximately 16–18 million and 94–106 million years ago (Ma), respectively; the three polyploidization events in <i>Ceratophyllum</i> were estimated to occur approximately 13–15 Ma, 127–143 Ma and 157–177 Ma, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1b</a>). Furthermore, we identified polyploidization events in the genomes of <i>C. kanehirae</i>, <i>P. americana</i>, <i>L. chinense</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. Interestingly, the <i>Cinnamomum</i> and <i>Persea</i> genomes share two recent polyploidization events, and multiple independent polyploidization events have occurred in each of five major clades (that is, Nymphaeales, magnoliids, monocots, Ceratophyllales and eudicots; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">7</a>), paralleling recent studies demonstrating that whole-genome duplication (WGD) is a widespread and potentially important evolutionary feature in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Estep, M. C. et al. Allopolyploidy, diversification, and the Miocene grassland expansion. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 15149–15154 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1003">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Cai, L. et al. Widespread ancient whole-genome duplications in Malpighiales coincide with Eocene global climatic upheaval. N. Phytol. 221, 565–576 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1006">31</a></sup>. To better elucidate the polyploidy of our newly assembled genomes, we conducted a more focused comparative genomic analysis using <i>Amborella</i>, <i>Cinnamomum</i>, <i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Vitis</i> as placeholders. Syntenic depth ratios of 6:1, 6:4, 6:2 and 6:3 were inferred in the <i>Euryale</i>–<i>Amborella</i>, <i>Euryale</i>–<i>Cinnamomum</i>, <i>Euryale</i>–<i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Euryale</i>–<i>Vitis</i> comparisons, respectively, and 8:1, 8:4, 8:2 and 8:3 in the <i>Ceratophyllum</i>–<i>Amborella</i>, <i>Ceratophyllum</i>–<i>Cinnamomum, Ceratophyllum</i>–<i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Ceratophyllum</i>–<i>Vitis</i> comparisons, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1c</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">9</a>). On the basis of the syntenic relationships between and within each species, our analyses collectively demonstrate that <i>Euryale</i> underwent an ancient WGD followed by one whole-genome triplication, and <i>Ceratophyllum</i> has undergone three WGDs.</p><p>For the first time, the genomic taxon sampling represents two of the three orders in the ANA grade and four of the five subclades of Mesangiospermae. To resolve early angiosperm phylogeny, a total of 1,374 single-copy nuclear genes (SSCGs) were first identified with SonicParanoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cosentino, S. &amp; Iwasaki, W. SonicParanoid: fast, accurate and easy orthology inference. Bioinformatics 35, 149–151 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1089">32</a></sup> using whole-genome sequences from 14 seed plants—that is, four eudicots (<i>Aquilegia coerulea</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i> and <i>Vitis</i>), three monocots (<i>Musa acuminate</i>, <i>Oryza</i> and <i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>), three magnoliids (<i>Cinnamomum</i>, <i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Persea</i>), <i>Ceratophyllum</i>, two ANA-grade species (<i>Amborella</i> and <i>Euryale</i>) and one gymnosperm (<i>Ginkgo biloba</i>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">13</a>). Aligned protein-coding regions were concatenated and analysed using two methods—(1) including all three codon positions (SSCG-CDS) and (2) including only the first and second codon positions (SSCG-Codon12). Moreover, for coalescent-based analyses, gene trees were individually estimated from each of the two datasets (SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12), which were then input into ASTRAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhang, C., Rabiee, M., Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. ASTRAL-III: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Bioinform. 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1140">33</a></sup> for species tree inference (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">10</a>). Our estimated gene trees are generally well supported (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">11</a>), and both concatenation and coalescent analyses produced an identical strongly supported topology (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2a,b</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">14</a>). Here, <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Euryale</i> were placed as successively sister to all other angiosperms, and monocots and magnoliids were inferred as successively sister to <i>Ceratophyllum</i> and eudicots. Our phylogenetic placement of magnoliids differs from APG, but is consistent with other studies that used various molecular markers, such as the plastid inverted repeat region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Moore, M. J. et al. Phylogenetic analysis of the plastid inverted repeat for 244 species: insights into deeper-level angiosperm relationships from a long, slowly evolving sequence region. Int. J. Plant Sci. 172, 541–558 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1170">34</a></sup> and transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1174">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="One Thousand Plant Transcriptomes Initiative One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1177">35</a></sup>. To avoid potential errors in orthology inference, we also extracted single-copy genes using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1181">36</a></sup> from the 14 seed plants described above as well as another gymnosperm (<i>Picea abies</i>). Only those genes sampled from at least 11 species were selected for downstream analyses, and a total of 2,302 single-copy genes (OSCG) were retained with an average of 1,859 genes for each species. Concatenation and coalescent analyses were similarly conducted as got those described above, and corroborated our phylogenetic findings (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">15</a>). Furthermore, we took advantage of the newly developed species-tree inference method STAG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Emms, D. &amp; Kelly, S. STAG: species tree inference from all genes. Preprint at &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/267914&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1195">37</a></sup>, which was designed to leverage gene trees estimated from multi-copy gene families. Only those genes sampled from all 15 seed plants were included, and a total of 2,356 low-copy genes (LCG) were retained. Species trees inferred from datasets including all three codon positions (LCG-CDS) and the first and second codon positions only (LCG-Codon12) were topologically identical to the ones described above (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">16</a>), suggesting that our findings are robust.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenomic analyses of early-diverging angiosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Phylogenomic analyses of early-diverging angiosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="634" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Chronogram of early-diverging angiosperms on the basis of the dataset SSCG-CDS inferred using MCMCTree. Bootstrap support percentages and posterior probabilities are indicated for each internal branch (from left to right, SSCG-CDS concatenation analysis using maximum likelihood (CA-ML), SSCG-CDS ASTRAL, LCG-CDS STAG, SSCG-Codon12 CA-ML, SSCG-Codon12 ASTRAL, LCG-Codon12 STAG), and an asterisk indicates 100 bootstrap support percentages and 1.0 posterior probabilities in all analyses. i, ii and iii indicate each internal branch. C, Carboniferous; J, Jurassic; K, Cretaceous; N, Neogene; P, Permian; <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw7/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Figa_HTML.jpg" style="width:7px;max-width:none;" alt="" />, Triassic; Q, Quaternary; <img src="//media.springernature.com/lw9/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-020-0594-6/MediaObjects/41477_2020_594_Figb_HTML.jpg" style="width:9px;max-width:none;" alt="" />, Palaeogene. <b>b</b>, Species tree analysis using DiscoVista. Rows correspond to focal splits, and the spectrum indicates the support value for splits that are compatible with a species tree. Teal indicates themonophyly of a clade, and the different shades of teal indicate the level of its bootstrap support percentage (0 to 100%). Orange indicates rejection of a clade, and a 95% cut-off (instead of astandard 75%) was selected for strong rejection due to higher support values with genome-scale data. <b>c</b>, Superimposed ultrametric gene trees in a consensus DensiTree plot. The datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12 are shown in red and orange, respectively. <b>d</b>, The frequency of three topologies (q1–q3) around focal internal branches of ASTRAL species trees in the datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12. Each internal branch (labelled i, ii and iii) with four neighbouring branches can lead to three possible topologies (for example, q1, q2 and q3). <b>e</b>, Gene tree compatibility. The portion of gene trees for which focal splits are highly (or weakly) supported (or rejected). Weakly rejected splits are those that are not in the tree but are compatible if low support branches (below 75%) are contracted.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Despite the fact that the same set of phylogenetic relationships was consistently recovered when nuclear genes were analysed simultaneously, topological conflicts among gene trees were widely observed as visualized using DensiTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bouckaert, R. R. DensiTree: making sense of sets of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 26, 1372–1373 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1238">38</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2c</a>). A major discordance was identified in the datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12 involving the relationship between <i>Amborella</i> and Nymphaeales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2c</a>). For the datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12, 46.3% and 44.5% of all 1,374 gene trees supported <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Euryale</i> as successively sister to all other angiosperms, respectively; 27.2% and 26.0% supported <i>Amborella</i> as sister to <i>Euryale</i>, respectively; and the other 26.5% and 29.5% supported <i>Euryale</i> alone as the first lineage of angiosperms, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">17</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">19</a>). We also summarized gene tree discordance using DiscoVista<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Sayyari, E., Whitfield, J. B. &amp; Mirarab, S. DiscoVista: interpretable visualizations of gene tree discordance. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 122, 110–115 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1277">39</a></sup>, and similar results were observed for the datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12—that is, a substantial fraction of gene trees were incongruent with species trees regarding the placement of <i>Amborella</i> and Nymphaeales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2e</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">17</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">19</a>). Moreover, conflicting phylogenetic placements of Ceratophyllales were observed in the gene trees. For the datasets SSCG-CDS and SSCG-Codon12, 39.7% and 34.2% of 1,374 gene trees supported <i>Ceratophyllum</i> as sister to eudicots, respectively; 27.6% and 32.9% supported <i>Ceratophyllum</i> as sister to monocots, respectively; and the other 32.7% and 32.9% supported <i>Ceratophyllum</i> as sister to magnoliids, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">17</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">19</a>). These analyses indicate that there is probably substantial ILS during early angiosperm evolution and greatly highlight the phylogenomic complexity of resolving early-diverging angiosperms.</p><p>Furthermore, phylogenetic analyses of these 15 seed plants inferred from 72 concatenated plastid genes strongly support magnoliids as the first diverging lineage of Mesangiospermae (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">20</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">14</a>). This placement of magnoliids is incongruent with our nuclear phylogeny, but consistent with a recent study that analysed 2,881 plastomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Li, H. T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461–470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1321">4</a></sup>. Thus, what might account for this deep phylogenetic incongruence between nuclear and plastid genomes? As multiple independent polyploidization events were identified in magnoliids, monocots, Ceratophyllales and eudicots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#Fig1">1</a>), allopolyploidization or hybridization is one probable source of genomic discordance. We first assessed putative hybridization events in our phylogeny using PhyloNetworks. Although three cases of hybridization were inferred, none involved the three species of magnoliids (that is, <i>Cinnamomum</i>, <i>Liriodendron</i> and <i>Persea</i>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">21</a>). Furthermore, very short internal branches among four subclades of Mesangiospermae were observed in all our analyses, corresponding to an estimated divergence time of around 20 Ma (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">22</a>). We therefore tested whether ILS might better explain this discordance. We simulated 20,000 gene trees under the multispecies coalescent model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Rannala, B. &amp; Yang, Z. H. Bayes estimation of species divergence times and ancestral population sizes using DNA sequences from multiple loci. Genetics 164, 1645–1656 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1354">40</a></sup> on the basis of the ASTRAL tree inferred from the dataset SSCG-CDS. We found considerable agreement between simulated and empirical gene trees (overall correlation coefficient, Spearman’s <i>ρ</i> = 0.97, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">23</a>), suggesting that the multispecies coalescent model is a good fit to our data. Here the relative frequencies of various topologies, including the topology inferred from plastomes, were consistent with frequencies of ILS as estimated from our coalescent analyses (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">23c,d</a>). These results indicate that ILS may well account for the incongruent placement of magnoliids between nuclear and plastid genomes. Finally, as sparse taxon sampling could result in these discordant results<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hillis, D. M., Pollock, D. D., McGuire, J. A. &amp; Zwickl, D. J. Is sparse taxon sampling a problem for phylogenetic inference? Syst. Biol. 52, 124–126 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1371">41</a></sup>, we increased our taxon sampling in the nuclear phylogeny by adding taxa with published genomes. A total of 612 ‘mostly’ single-copy orthologous genes (SCOG) were extracted from 213 nuclear genomes, which included 211 angiosperms representing 33 orders and 67 families as well as two gymnosperms as outgroups (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">15</a>), and the average number of genes per taxon was 545. Coalescent analyses of the datasets SCOG-CDS and SCOG-Codon12 recovered the same relationships among the four subclades of Mesangiospermae (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">24</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">26</a>), suggesting that our results are robust to additional taxa sampling.</p><p>In summary, the high-quality genomes of prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort greatly help to clarify phylogenetic relationships of early-diverging angiosperms. Moreover, these genomic resources are essential for future comparative investigations of genic evolution that underpin the morphological, physiological and ecological diversification of early angiosperms (Supplementary Notes <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">1–3</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">27</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">28</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">16</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">22</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant materials and DNA sequencing</h3><p>Fresh leaves of <i>E. ferox</i> and whole plants of <i>C. demersum</i> were obtained for DNA extraction and sequencing. Total genomic DNA was extracted using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Tel-Zur, N., Abbo, S., Myslabodski, D. &amp; Mizrahi, Y. Modified CTAB procedure for DNA isolation from epiphytic cacti of the genera Hylocereus and Selenicereus (Cactaceae). Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 17, 249–254 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1420">42</a></sup>. The library for ONT sequencing was constructed using large (&gt;20 kb) DNA fragments with the Ligation Sequencing Kit 1D (SQK-LSK108), and sequenced using the GridION X5 platform. Adapters and low-quality nucleotides (that is, mean quality score &lt;7) were trimmed. Paired-end libraries with an insertion size of 350 bp were constructed according to the manufacturer’s protocols and sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 System. Illumina reads were filtered using following criteria: (1) containing more than 5% unidentified nucleotides, (2) more than 65% of bases with a Phred quality score &lt;7 and (3) more than 10 bp adapter sequences (allowing 2 bp mismatches). For the high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) analysis, fresh leaves were fixed in formaldehyde solution (1%), and chromatin was cross-linked and digested using the restriction enzyme HindIII. The 5′ overhangs were filled-in with biotinylated nucleotides, and free blunt ends were ligated. After ligation, crosslinks were reversed and the DNA was purified to remove protein. Purified DNA was treated to remove biotin that was not internal to ligated fragments. DNA was sheared into fragments of ~350 bp, and sequenced using the Illumina platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome size estimation</h3><p>Genome size was estimated using the <i>k</i>-mer analysis of Illumina 150-bp paired-end reads. The <i>k</i>-mer depth-frequency distribution was generated using SOAPec<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1438">43</a></sup> (v.2.0.1, <a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/</a>) with the following parameters: -k 17 -q 33 -t 10. The genome size was then calculated according to the following formula<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Shi, J. et al. Chromosome conformation capture resolved near complete genome assembly of broomcorn millet. Nat. Commun. 10, 464 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1446">44</a></sup>: genome size = <i>k</i>-mer coverage/mean <i>k</i>-mer depth (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly</h3><p>ONT long reads were de novo assembled using the Canu assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1468">25</a></sup> (v.1.7, <a href="https://github.com/marbl/canu/">https://github.com/marbl/canu/</a>), and two rounds of polishing were applied to the assembled contigs using Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1476">26</a></sup> (v.1.22, <a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon/">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon/</a>) with the Illumina short reads. HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1484">45</a></sup> v.2.10.0 was used to evaluate the quality of Hi-C data. Valid interaction pairs were mapped to the contigs and anchored to the pseudochromosomes using LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1489">27</a></sup> (<a href="https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS">https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transcriptome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>For each species, total RNA was extracted from various plant organs (roots, leaves and stems), and residual DNA was removed using the DNA-free DNA Removal Kit. A total of 18.69 Gb and 5.85 Gb of reads were generated using the Illumina platform for <i>E. ferox</i> and <i>C. demersum</i>, respectively. Transcripts were assembled from filtered reads using Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1511">46</a></sup> v.2.8.4 with additional parameters including ‘--trimmomatic --normalize_reads’.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Annotation of repetitive elements</h3><p>To annotate repetitive elements, we utilized a combination of evidence-based and de novo approaches. Genome assemblies were first searched using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1524">47</a></sup> (v.4.0.7, <a href="http://repeatmasker.org/">http://repeatmasker.org/</a>) against the Repbase database (<a href="http://www.girinst.org/repbase">http://www.girinst.org/repbase</a>). Next, a de novo repetitive-element library was constructed using RepeatModeler (v.1.0.11, <a href="http://repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>). This de novo repetitive-element library was then utilized by RepeatMasker to annotate repetitive elements. Results from these two runs of RepeatMasker were merged.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Protein-coding gene prediction and functional annotation</h3><p>The identification of protein-coding genes was based on transcriptome data and ab initio prediction. RNA transcripts were first mapped to the assembled genome using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1548">48</a></sup> (Program to Assemble Spliced Alignment v.2.3.3). Valid transcript alignments were clustered on the basis of mapping location and assembled into gene structures, and then the high-quality gene models were selected for training by AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1552">49</a></sup> v.3.2.3. Moreover, intron hints were generated using the script bam2hints provided by AUGUSTUS. Next, AUGUSTUS was utilized for ab initio gene prediction on the hard-masked genome assembly, and all of the predictions were integrated using EvidenceModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1556">49</a></sup> (EVM, v.1.1.1) to generate consensus gene sets. For functional annotation, our predicted protein-coding genes were searched against the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases, as well as the InterPro database using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1560">50</a></sup> release 5.33–72.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Polyploidization analysis</h3><p>Seven genomes were selected for our polyploidization analysis, that is, <i>A. trichopoda</i> (Amborellales; At), <i>C. demersum</i> (Ceratophyllales), <i>C. kanehirae</i> (magnoliids), <i>E. ferox</i> (Nymphaeales; Ef), <i>L. chinense</i> (magnoliids), <i>O. sativa</i> (monocots), <i>P. Americana</i> (magnoliids) and <i>V</i>. <i>vinifera</i> (eudicots; Vv). Colinear genes within each genome and between genomes were inferred using MCScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Tang, H. B. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1600">51</a></sup> v.0.8 according to the combined information of gene similarity and gene order. Synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (Ks) between colinear genes were estimated using the Nei–Gojobori approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418–426 (1986)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1604">52</a></sup> as implemented in the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1609">53</a></sup> package v.4.9 h. The median Ks values were selected to represent each syntenic block, and the probability density distribution curve of Ks was estimated using MATLAB with the kernel smoothing density function (ksdensity; bandwidth was typically set to 0.025). Multipeak fitting of the curve was performed using the Gaussian approximation function (cftool) in MATLAB, and the coefficient of determination (<i>R</i><sup>2</sup>) was set as at least 0.95.</p><p>Furthermore, we performed a correction to the Ks values to distinguish the order of each polyploidization event using a similar method to a method used previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wang, X. et al. Genome alignment spanning major Poaceae lineages reveals heterogeneous evolutionary rates and alters inferred dates for key evolutionary events. Mol. Plant 8, 885–898 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-020-0594-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d12344e1620">54</a></sup>. Here, supposing that the Ks values of colinear orthologues between two genomes <i>i</i> and <i>j</i> are <span class="mathjax-tex">\(X_{i - j}:N\left( {\mu _{i - j},\sigma _{i - j}^2} \right)\)</span>, where <i>N</i> represents the normal distribution, <i>μ</i> represents the mean value and <i>σ</i> represents the standard deviation. We further supposing that the ratio of the evolutionary rate of species <i>i</i> to the assumed averaged evolutionary rate of angiosperms is <i>r</i><sub><i>i</i></sub>, the correction coefficient <i>λ</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> is defined as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\lambda _i = \frac{1}{{r_i}}\)</span> and, accordingly, the correction coefficient factor of <i>X</i><sub><i>i</i> <i>−</i> <i>j</i></sub> is defined as <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\lambda _{ij} = \lambda _i \lambda _j\)</span></p><p>The mean of the corrected <i>X</i><sub><i>i</i> <i>−</i> <i>j-correction</i></sub> can be inferred to be</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\upmu}_{{\mathrm{i}} - {\mathrm{j}} - {\mathrm{correction}}} = \mu _{i - j}\lambda _i \lambda _j$$</span></div></div><p>For <i>E</i>[<i>tX</i>] = <i>tE</i>[<i>X</i>] and <i>D</i>[<i>tX</i>] = <i>t</i><sup>2</sup><i>D</i>[<i>X</i>]</p><p>we can get</p><div id="Equb" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$X_{i - j - {\mathrm{correction}}}:N\left( {\mu _{i - j - {\mathrm{correction}}},\sigma _{i - j - correction}^2} \right) = N\left( {\lambda _i\lambda _j\mu _{i - j},\lambda _i^2\lambda _j^2\sigma _{i - j}^2} \right)$$</span></div></div><p>As <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Euryale</i> are basal angiosperms, the divergence between <i>Amborella</i> or <i>Euryale</i> and other plants occurred at the same time. Therefore, for genome <i>i</i></p><div id="Equc" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - i - {\mathrm{correction}}}}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - {\mathrm{Vv}} - {\mathrm{correction}}}}} = \frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - i} \lambda _{{\mathrm{At}}}\lambda _i}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}}\lambda _{{\mathrm{At}}}\lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}}}} = \frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - i} \lambda _i}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}} \lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}}}} = 1$$</span></div></div><div id="Equd" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - i - {\mathrm{correction}}}}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - {\mathrm{Vv}} - {\mathrm{correction}}}}} = \frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - i} \lambda _{{\mathrm{Ef}}} \lambda _i}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}} \lambda _{{\mathrm{Ef}}} \lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}}}} = \frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - i} \lambda _i}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}} \lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}}}} = 1$$</span></div></div><div id="Eque" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\frac{{\lambda _i}}{{\lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}}}} = a_i = {\mathrm{mean}}\left\{ {\frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}}}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{At}} - i}}},\frac{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - {\mathrm{Vv}}}}}{{\mu _{{\mathrm{Ef}} - i}}}} \right\}$$</span></div></div><div id="Equf" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$\lambda _i = \lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}} a_i$$</span></div></div><p>The <i>a</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> represents the mean ratio value among the observed Ks peak between <i>Amboralla</i> and <i>Vitis</i>, or <i>Euryale</i> and <i>Vitis</i>. After the divergence from the other studied plants, <i>A</i>. <i>trichopoda</i> has not been affected by polyploidization anymore; thus, we assumed that the evolutionary rate of <i>Amborella</i> genes is relatively stable and, therefore, set <i>λ</i><sub>At</sub> = 1. The plant <i>i</i> with the slowest evolutionary rate is the most likely to have the same evolutionary rate as <i>Amborella</i>, that is, <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\max \left\{ {\lambda _i} \right\} = 1\)</span> and <span class="mathjax-tex">\(\lambda _{{\mathrm{Vv}}} = \frac{{\max \left\{ {\lambda _i} \right\}}}{{\max \left\{ {a_i} \right\}}} = \frac{1}{{\max \left\{ {a_i} \right\}}}\)</span>.
